Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Any mistake or objection against a caliph automatically invalidates his imamate.
Correction: The author shows that many objections are either fabrications, matters of ijtihad, or do not affect the imamate; and even if true, some would blame the Companions, not the caliph.

Checkpoint
According to the author, what would be the implication if the Companions truly abandoned Uthman without excuse?
A) It would prove Uthman was unworthy.
B) It would be a defect in the Companions, not Uthman.
C) It would invalidate the caliphate.
D) It would be a sign of Uthman's tyranny.

Key Takeaway
The author defends Uthman's imamate by showing that criticisms are either harmless, fabricated, or matters of ijtihad, and any fault would lie with the Companions, not him.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hear criticisms of a leader you respect, do you immediately assume the worst, or do you consider that some allegations might be fabricated or matters of ijtihad? How can you train yourself to not judge without full context?
?
This lesson shows that abandonment by companions could be a defect in them, not the caliph. Are there times when you have abandoned someone in need, assuming they deserved it? What will you do differently now?
?
The author says some objections do not harm imamate. Are there areas in your life where you let small flaws in leaders excuse you from obedience or respect? What can you do to correct that?

The Accusations against Uthman
Lists accusations against Uthman (رضي الله عنه).

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the main accusations leveled against Caliph Uthman (رضي الله عنه) and how they are refuted in Sunni theology.

Core Idea
Accusations against Uthman are answered through rational and textual arguments, not blind defense.

This lesson presents the core accusations against Uthman (رضي الله عنه) as recorded in the text, providing a foundation for understanding the refutations that follow. It is essential to know these objections to appreciate the scholarly response.

Guided Path
1
Read the accusations list
Carefully read the list of accusations against Uthman. Note the variety: appointments, financial matters, corporal punishment, and abandonment.
This list is comprehensive.
2
Categorize the accusations
Group the accusations into categories: governance, financial, physical harm, legal decisions, and abandonment.
The refutation will address each category separately.
3
Identify the tone
Notice that the accusations are presented as claims, not established facts. The refutation will challenge their validity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
من مطاعنهم في عثمان (رضي الله عنه) أنه ولى أمور المسلمين من ظهر منهم الفسق والفساد كالوليد بن عقبة وعبد الله بن أبي سرح ومروان بن الحكم، ومعاوية بن أبي سفيان ومن يجري مجراهم، وأنه صرف أموال بيت المال إلى أقاربه حتى نقل أنه صرف إلى أربعة نفر منهم أربعمائة ألف درهم، وأنه حمى لنفسه . وقد قال النبي (صلى الله عليه وسلم) إنه لا حمى إلا لله ولرسوله. وعمر إنما حمى حمى لابل المسلمين العاجزين ولنحو نعم الصدقة والجزية والضوال، لا لنفسه، وأنه أحرق مصحف ابن مسعود، وضربه حتى كسر ضلعين من أضلاعه، وضرب عماراً حتى أصابه فتق ، وضرب أبا ذر ونفاه إلى الربذة وأنه رد الحكم بن العاص وقد سيره رسول الله (صلى الله عليه وسلم) وأنه أسقط القود عن عبد الله بن عمر وقد قتل الهرمزان ، والحد عن الوليد بن عقبة وقد شرب الخمر وأن الصحابة خذلوه حتى قتل، ولم يدفن إلا بعد ثلاثة أيام.
والجواب أن بعض هذه الأمور مما لا يقدح في إمامته كظهور الفسق والفساد من ولاة بعض البلاد، إذ لا اطلاع له على السرائر، وإنما عليه الأخذ بالظاهر، والعزل عند تحقق الفسق ومعاوية كان على الشام في زمن عمر أيضاً، والمذهب أن الباغي ليس بفاسق ولو سلم، فإنما ظهر ذلك في زمان إمامة علي (رضي الله عنه). وبعضها افتراء محض، كصرف ذلك القدر من بيت المال إلى أقاربه، وأخذ الحمى لنفسه، وضرب الصحابة إلى الحد المذكور. وبعضها اجتهاديات مفوضة إلى رأي الإمام حسب ما يراه من المصلحة كالتأديب والتعزير، ودرء الحدود والقصاص بالشبهات والتأويلات. وبعضها كان بإذن النبي كرد الحكم بن العاص على ما روي أنه ذكر ذلك لأبي بكر وعمر (رضي الله عنهما) فقالا : إنك شاهد واحد. فلما آل الأمر إليه حكم بعلمه وأما حديث خذلان الصحابة إياه وتركهم دفنه من غير عذر، فلو صح، كان قدحاً فيهم لا فيه. ونحن لا نظن بالمهاجرين والأنصار (رضي الله عنهم) وبعلي بن أبي طالب (رضي الله عنه) خصوصاً أن يرضوا بقتل إمامهم مظلوم في دارهم وترك دفن ميت في جوارهم، سيما من هو قانت آناء الليل ساجداً وقائماً، وعاكف طول النهار، ذاكراً وصائماً، شرفه رسول الله بابنتيه، وبشره بالجنة، وأثنى عليه. فكيف يخذلونه، وقد كان من زمرتهم، وطول العمر في نصرتهم، وعلموا سابقته في الإسلام وخاتمته إلى دار السلام. لكنه لم يأذن لهم في المحاربة، ولم يرض بما حاولوا من المدافعة، تحامياً عن إراقة الدماء، ورضاً بسابق القضاء. ومع ذلك لم يدع الحسن والحسين (رضي الله عنهما) في الدفع عنه مقدوراً وكان أمر الله قدراً مقدوراً.
Among their objections against Uthman (may Allah be pleased with him) is that he appointed over the affairs of the Muslims those who showed corruption and depravity, such as al-Walid ibn 'Uqba, 'Abdullah ibn Abi Sarh, Marwan ibn al-Hakam, Mu'awiya ibn Abi Sufyan and those similar to them; and that he spent money from the treasury on his relatives until it is reported that he gave to four of them four hundred thousand dirhams; and that he reserved pasturage for himself. The Prophet (peace be upon him) said: 'There is no reserved pasturage except for Allah and His Messenger.' 'Umar only reserved pasturage for the weak Muslims and for camels of charity, jizya, and stray animals, not for himself. And that he burned the mushaf of Ibn Mas'ud, and beat him until he broke two of his ribs; and he beat 'Ammar until he developed a hernia; and he beat Abu Dharr and exiled him to al-Rabadha; and that he brought back al-Hakam ibn al-'As whom the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) had exiled; and that he dropped the blood-money from 'Abdullah ibn 'Umar after he killed al-Hurmuzan, and the prescribed punishment from al-Walid ibn 'Uqba after he drank wine; and that the Companions abandoned him until he was killed, and he was not buried except after three days.

The answer is that some of these matters do not harm his imamate, such as the appearance of corruption and depravity from the governors of some regions, for he had no knowledge of their inner states; he only had to act according to the apparent, and to dismiss them when corruption was confirmed. And Mu'awiya was over Syria in the time of 'Umar as well, and the school [of thought] is that the rebel is not a sinner. Even if it were granted, that appeared only during the imamate of 'Ali (may Allah be pleased with him). Some of them are pure fabrication, such as spending that amount from the treasury on his relatives, taking reserved pasturage for himself, and beating the Companions to the mentioned extent. Some of them are ijtihad matters delegated to the opinion of the imam according to what he sees as beneficial, such as discipline and discretionary punishment, and averting prescribed punishments and retaliation due to doubts and interpretations. Some of them were with the permission of the Prophet, like bringing back al-Hakam ibn al-'As, based on the report that he mentioned that to Abu Bakr and 'Umar (may Allah be pleased with them) and they said: 'You are a single witness.' So when the matter came to him, he ruled based on his knowledge. As for the report of the Companions abandoning him and leaving his burial without excuse, if it were true, it would be a defect in them, not in him. And we do not think that the Emigrants and Helpers (may Allah be pleased with them all), and especially 'Ali ibn Abi Talib (may Allah be pleased with him), that they would be pleased with the killing of their imam, oppressed in their land, and the leaving of a dead person unburied in their vicinity, especially one who is devout in the hours of the night, prostrating and standing, and constant throughout the day, remembering and fasting; the Messenger of Allah honored him by giving him his two daughters, gave him glad tidings of Paradise, and praised him. So how could they abandon him, when he was from their group, and had lived long in supporting them, and they knew his precedence in Islam and his ending in the abode of peace? But he did not permit them to fight, and he was not pleased with what they attempted of defense, out of caution against shedding blood, and satisfaction with the prior decree. Despite that, al-Hasan and al-Husayn (may Allah be pleased with them) did not leave any possible effort in defending him, and the command of Allah was a determined decree.
Source pages p. 285

Commentary
This passage is a detailed objection-and-answer. The author first enumerates several accusations against Caliph Uthman (may Allah be pleased with him): appointing corrupt governors, misusing treasury funds, reserving pasturage for himself, burning the mushaf of Ibn Mas'ud, beating companions, exiling Abu Dharr, returning al-Hakam ibn al-'As who was exiled by the Prophet, dropping prescribed punishments, and the abandonment by the Companions. The refutation categorizes the accusations into four groups: (1) Some do not harm his imamate, like appointing governors who appeared outwardly just but later became corrupt; the imam judges by the apparent and dismisses when corruption is proven. Mu'awiya was appointed by 'Umar as well, and the school holds that a rebel (bāghī) is not a sinner (fāsiq). (2) Some are pure fabrications, like the specific large sums to relatives, the reserved pasturage, and the severe beatings. (3) Some are matters of ijtihad left to the imam's judgment for discipline and averting punishments due to legal doubts. (4) Some were done with the Prophet's permission, like returning al-Hakam, as the Prophet had said that two witnesses are needed for exile but 'Uthman was a single witness. As for the abandonment, if true, it would reflect on the Companions, not Uthman; the author argues it is inconceivable that the best Companions, especially 'Ali, would abandon such a virtuous caliph, but they refrained from fighting to avoid bloodshed, and the two sons of 'Ali did all they could. The conclusion emphasizes divine decree.

Key Takeaway
The accusations against Uthman are serious but are later refuted through various rational and textual arguments.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hear accusations against a Companion of the Prophet (SAW), what is your immediate reaction? Do you assume guilt or innocence? How does reading this list of unrefuted accusations challenge you to suspend judgment until you hear both sides?
?
Uthman (RA) endured all these allegations without retaliation. Think of a time you were unfairly accused. How did you respond? Does this lesson teach you anything about patience and trust in Allah's justice that you can apply in your own life?
?
These accusations led to Uthman's murder and delayed burial. Are there people in your life you have 'killed' with your tongue through slander or assumption? What specific step will you take this week to seek forgiveness and restore their honor?

Refutation: Appointing Governors and Mu'awiya
Refutes the accusation of appointing corrupt governors.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the accusation of appointing corrupt governors does not undermine Uthman's imamate, including the case of Mu'awiya.

Core Idea
An imam judges by outward appearances and dismisses governors only when corruption is confirmed.

This lesson focuses on the first category of refutation: accusations related to Uthman's appointment of governors. The author argues that such appointments do not harm his imamate because the imam can only act on apparent righteousness and dismiss when corruption becomes clear.

Guided Path
1
Read the refutation portion
Read the section that begins with 'والجواب أن بعض هذه الأمور مما لا يقدح في إمامته' and continues through the mention of Mu'awiya.
2
Understand the principle
The principle is that the imam is not responsible for hidden sins of appointees; he acts on the apparent. Corruption after appointment requires dismissal, which Uthman could have done later.
This is a standard fiqh principle.
3
Consider the case of Mu'awiya
Mu'awiya was appointed by Umar as well, and the school holds that a rebel (bāghī) is not a fāsiq (sinner). Plus, his rebellion became clear only in Ali's time.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
من مطاعنهم في عثمان (رضي الله عنه) أنه ولى أمور المسلمين من ظهر منهم الفسق والفساد كالوليد بن عقبة وعبد الله بن أبي سرح ومروان بن الحكم، ومعاوية بن أبي سفيان ومن يجري مجراهم، وأنه صرف أموال بيت المال إلى أقاربه حتى نقل أنه صرف إلى أربعة نفر منهم أربعمائة ألف درهم، وأنه حمى لنفسه . وقد قال النبي (صلى الله عليه وسلم) إنه لا حمى إلا لله ولرسوله. وعمر إنما حمى حمى لابل المسلمين العاجزين ولنحو نعم الصدقة والجزية والضوال، لا لنفسه، وأنه أحرق مصحف ابن مسعود، وضربه حتى كسر ضلعين من أضلاعه، وضرب عماراً حتى أصابه فتق ، وضرب أبا ذر ونفاه إلى الربذة وأنه رد الحكم بن العاص وقد سيره رسول الله (صلى الله عليه وسلم) وأنه أسقط القود عن عبد الله بن عمر وقد قتل الهرمزان ، والحد عن الوليد بن عقبة وقد شرب الخمر وأن الصحابة خذلوه حتى قتل، ولم يدفن إلا بعد ثلاثة أيام.
والجواب أن بعض هذه الأمور مما لا يقدح في إمامته كظهور الفسق والفساد من ولاة بعض البلاد، إذ لا اطلاع له على السرائر، وإنما عليه الأخذ بالظاهر، والعزل عند تحقق الفسق ومعاوية كان على الشام في زمن عمر أيضاً، والمذهب أن الباغي ليس بفاسق ولو سلم، فإنما ظهر ذلك في زمان إمامة علي (رضي الله عنه). وبعضها افتراء محض، كصرف ذلك القدر من بيت المال إلى أقاربه، وأخذ الحمى لنفسه، وضرب الصحابة إلى الحد المذكور. وبعضها اجتهاديات مفوضة إلى رأي الإمام حسب ما يراه من المصلحة كالتأديب والتعزير، ودرء الحدود والقصاص بالشبهات والتأويلات. وبعضها كان بإذن النبي كرد الحكم بن العاص على ما روي أنه ذكر ذلك لأبي بكر وعمر (رضي الله عنهما) فقالا : إنك شاهد واحد. فلما آل الأمر إليه حكم بعلمه وأما حديث خذلان الصحابة إياه وتركهم دفنه من غير عذر، فلو صح، كان قدحاً فيهم لا فيه. ونحن لا نظن بالمهاجرين والأنصار (رضي الله عنهم) وبعلي بن أبي طالب (رضي الله عنه) خصوصاً أن يرضوا بقتل إمامهم مظلوم في دارهم وترك دفن ميت في جوارهم، سيما من هو قانت آناء الليل ساجداً وقائماً، وعاكف طول النهار، ذاكراً وصائماً، شرفه رسول الله بابنتيه، وبشره بالجنة، وأثنى عليه. فكيف يخذلونه، وقد كان من زمرتهم، وطول العمر في نصرتهم، وعلموا سابقته في الإسلام وخاتمته إلى دار السلام. لكنه لم يأذن لهم في المحاربة، ولم يرض بما حاولوا من المدافعة، تحامياً عن إراقة الدماء، ورضاً بسابق القضاء. ومع ذلك لم يدع الحسن والحسين (رضي الله عنهما) في الدفع عنه مقدوراً وكان أمر الله قدراً مقدوراً.
Among their objections against Uthman (may Allah be pleased with him) is that he appointed over the affairs of the Muslims those who showed corruption and depravity, such as al-Walid ibn 'Uqba, 'Abdullah ibn Abi Sarh, Marwan ibn al-Hakam, Mu'awiya ibn Abi Sufyan and those similar to them; and that he spent money from the treasury on his relatives until it is reported that he gave to four of them four hundred thousand dirhams; and that he reserved pasturage for himself. The Prophet (peace be upon him) said: 'There is no reserved pasturage except for Allah and His Messenger.' 'Umar only reserved pasturage for the weak Muslims and for camels of charity, jizya, and stray animals, not for himself. And that he burned the mushaf of Ibn Mas'ud, and beat him until he broke two of his ribs; and he beat 'Ammar until he developed a hernia; and he beat Abu Dharr and exiled him to al-Rabadha; and that he brought back al-Hakam ibn al-'As whom the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) had exiled; and that he dropped the blood-money from 'Abdullah ibn 'Umar after he killed al-Hurmuzan, and the prescribed punishment from al-Walid ibn 'Uqba after he drank wine; and that the Companions abandoned him until he was killed, and he was not buried except after three days.

The answer is that some of these matters do not harm his imamate, such as the appearance of corruption and depravity from the governors of some regions, for he had no knowledge of their inner states; he only had to act according to the apparent, and to dismiss them when corruption was confirmed. And Mu'awiya was over Syria in the time of 'Umar as well, and the school [of thought] is that the rebel is not a sinner. Even if it were granted, that appeared only during the imamate of 'Ali (may Allah be pleased with him). Some of them are pure fabrication, such as spending that amount from the treasury on his relatives, taking reserved pasturage for himself, and beating the Companions to the mentioned extent. Some of them are ijtihad matters delegated to the opinion of the imam according to what he sees as beneficial, such as discipline and discretionary punishment, and averting prescribed punishments and retaliation due to doubts and interpretations. Some of them were with the permission of the Prophet, like bringing back al-Hakam ibn al-'As, based on the report that he mentioned that to Abu Bakr and 'Umar (may Allah be pleased with them) and they said: 'You are a single witness.' So when the matter came to him, he ruled based on his knowledge. As for the report of the Companions abandoning him and leaving his burial without excuse, if it were true, it would be a defect in them, not in him. And we do not think that the Emigrants and Helpers (may Allah be pleased with them all), and especially 'Ali ibn Abi Talib (may Allah be pleased with him), that they would be pleased with the killing of their imam, oppressed in their land, and the leaving of a dead person unburied in their vicinity, especially one who is devout in the hours of the night, prostrating and standing, and constant throughout the day, remembering and fasting; the Messenger of Allah honored him by giving him his two daughters, gave him glad tidings of Paradise, and praised him. So how could they abandon him, when he was from their group, and had lived long in supporting them, and they knew his precedence in Islam and his ending in the abode of peace? But he did not permit them to fight, and he was not pleased with what they attempted of defense, out of caution against shedding blood, and satisfaction with the prior decree. Despite that, al-Hasan and al-Husayn (may Allah be pleased with them) did not leave any possible effort in defending him, and the command of Allah was a determined decree.
Source pages p. 285

Commentary
This passage is a detailed objection-and-answer. The author first enumerates several accusations against Caliph Uthman (may Allah be pleased with him): appointing corrupt governors, misusing treasury funds, reserving pasturage for himself, burning the mushaf of Ibn Mas'ud, beating companions, exiling Abu Dharr, returning al-Hakam ibn al-'As who was exiled by the Prophet, dropping prescribed punishments, and the abandonment by the Companions. The refutation categorizes the accusations into four groups: (1) Some do not harm his imamate, like appointing governors who appeared outwardly just but later became corrupt; the imam judges by the apparent and dismisses when corruption is proven. Mu'awiya was appointed by 'Umar as well, and the school holds that a rebel (bāghī) is not a sinner (fāsiq). (2) Some are pure fabrications, like the specific large sums to relatives, the reserved pasturage, and the severe beatings. (3) Some are matters of ijtihad left to the imam's judgment for discipline and averting punishments due to legal doubts. (4) Some were done with the Prophet's permission, like returning al-Hakam, as the Prophet had said that two witnesses are needed for exile but 'Uthman was a single witness. As for the abandonment, if true, it would reflect on the Companions, not Uthman; the author argues it is inconceivable that the best Companions, especially 'Ali, would abandon such a virtuous caliph, but they refrained from fighting to avoid bloodshed, and the two sons of 'Ali did all they could. The conclusion emphasizes divine decree.

Key Terms
اجتهاد
باغي
فسق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the refutation, why does the appointment of corrupt governors not harm Uthman's imamate?
A) Because Uthman was forced to appoint them
B) Because the imam judges by appearances and dismisses when corruption is confirmed
C) Because the governors repented later
D) Because Uthman did not appoint them

Key Takeaway
The imam is only responsible for apparent righteousness in appointments and must dismiss when corruption is confirmed; thus Uthman's imamate remains intact.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hear criticism of a leader or authority figure, do you hastily assume guilt without considering the limits of their knowledge? How does this lesson challenge your instinct to judge based on incomplete information? Commit to withholding judgment until you have the full picture.
?
Have you ever been in a position of responsibility over others (even in a small way) and been blamed for something you could not have known? How does the principle that leaders are only accountable for the apparent change your view of such situations? Will you now be more merciful toward those in authority?
?
The lesson distinguishes between rebellion and sin. Is there a person or group you have automatically labeled as sinful or corrupt without understanding their theological or political context? Will you now pause to judge more fairly, as the madhhab teaches?

Refutation: Fabrications and Ijtihad Issues
Refutes fabrication accusations and explains ijtihad matters.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author refutes accusations that are pure fabrications or fall under ijtihad.

Core Idea
Some accusations are baseless fabrications, while others are valid ijtihad matters within the imam's authority.

This lesson covers two categories of refutation: (1) accusations that are pure fabrications – such as the specific amounts spent on relatives and the severe beatings – and (2) accusations that are matters of ijtihad, where the imam uses his discretion as permitted by Islamic law.

Guided Path
1
Identify the fabrications
Read the list of accusations the author identifies as pure fabrication: spending 400,000 dirhams on relatives, taking reserved pasturage, and the severe beatings of Ibn Mas'ud, 'Ammar, and Abu Dharr.
These are presented as unsubstantiated claims.
2
Understand ijtihad matters
Learn that disciplinary punishments (التأديب والتعزير) and averting prescribed punishments due to doubts (درء الحدود والقصاص بالشبهات) are within the imam's discretionary authority.
Ijtihad allows flexibility based on circumstances.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

Refutation: Permissions from Prophet and the Abandonment Issue
Refutes accusations regarding return of al-Hakam and Companions' abandonment.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the refutation for actions said to be permitted by the Prophet and the defense against the claim that the Companions abandoned Uthman.

Core Idea
Uthman's actions were sometimes based on prior prophetic permission, and the Companions did not abandon him but refrained from fighting to avoid bloodshed.

This final lesson covers the last two categories: actions that the author claims were done with the Prophet's permission, such as returning al-Hakam ibn al-'As, and the sensitive issue of the Companions' supposed abandonment of Uthman, which is argued to be a defect in them, not in Uthman.

Guided Path
1
Read the permission argument
Examine the story of al-Hakam ibn al-'As. The Prophet had exiled him, but later permitted his return? The author cites a report that the Prophet mentioned this to Abu Bakr and 'Umar, but they said a single witness is insufficient. Uthman later ruled based on his own knowledge.
This demonstrates that Uthman's action had a basis in prophetic tradition.
2
Understand the abandonment refutation
The author argues that if the report were true, it would reflect poorly on the Companions, not Uthman. Moreover, it is inconceivable that the best Companions, especially Ali, would abandon such a virtuous man. They refrained from fighting to prevent greater bloodshed.
This is a defense of both Uthman and the Companions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
من مطاعنهم في عثمان (رضي الله عنه) أنه ولى أمور المسلمين من ظهر منهم الفسق والفساد كالوليد بن عقبة وعبد الله بن أبي سرح ومروان بن الحكم، ومعاوية بن أبي سفيان ومن يجري مجراهم، وأنه صرف أموال بيت المال إلى أقاربه حتى نقل أنه صرف إلى أربعة نفر منهم أربعمائة ألف درهم، وأنه حمى لنفسه . وقد قال النبي (صلى الله عليه وسلم) إنه لا حمى إلا لله ولرسوله. وعمر إنما حمى حمى لابل المسلمين العاجزين ولنحو نعم الصدقة والجزية والضوال، لا لنفسه، وأنه أحرق مصحف ابن مسعود، وضربه حتى كسر ضلعين من أضلاعه، وضرب عماراً حتى أصابه فتق ، وضرب أبا ذر ونفاه إلى الربذة وأنه رد الحكم بن العاص وقد سيره رسول الله (صلى الله عليه وسلم) وأنه أسقط القود عن عبد الله بن عمر وقد قتل الهرمزان ، والحد عن الوليد بن عقبة وقد شرب الخمر وأن الصحابة خذلوه حتى قتل، ولم يدفن إلا بعد ثلاثة أيام.
والجواب أن بعض هذه الأمور مما لا يقدح في إمامته كظهور الفسق والفساد من ولاة بعض البلاد، إذ لا اطلاع له على السرائر، وإنما عليه الأخذ بالظاهر، والعزل عند تحقق الفسق ومعاوية كان على الشام في زمن عمر أيضاً، والمذهب أن الباغي ليس بفاسق ولو سلم، فإنما ظهر ذلك في زمان إمامة علي (رضي الله عنه). وبعضها افتراء محض، كصرف ذلك القدر من بيت المال إلى أقاربه، وأخذ الحمى لنفسه، وضرب الصحابة إلى الحد المذكور. وبعضها اجتهاديات مفوضة إلى رأي الإمام حسب ما يراه من المصلحة كالتأديب والتعزير، ودرء الحدود والقصاص بالشبهات والتأويلات. وبعضها كان بإذن النبي كرد الحكم بن العاص على ما روي أنه ذكر ذلك لأبي بكر وعمر (رضي الله عنهما) فقالا : إنك شاهد واحد. فلما آل الأمر إليه حكم بعلمه وأما حديث خذلان الصحابة إياه وتركهم دفنه من غير عذر، فلو صح، كان قدحاً فيهم لا فيه. ونحن لا نظن بالمهاجرين والأنصار (رضي الله عنهم) وبعلي بن أبي طالب (رضي الله عنه) خصوصاً أن يرضوا بقتل إمامهم مظلوم في دارهم وترك دفن ميت في جوارهم، سيما من هو قانت آناء الليل ساجداً وقائماً، وعاكف طول النهار، ذاكراً وصائماً، شرفه رسول الله بابنتيه، وبشره بالجنة، وأثنى عليه. فكيف يخذلونه، وقد كان من زمرتهم، وطول العمر في نصرتهم، وعلموا سابقته في الإسلام وخاتمته إلى دار السلام. لكنه لم يأذن لهم في المحاربة، ولم يرض بما حاولوا من المدافعة، تحامياً عن إراقة الدماء، ورضاً بسابق القضاء. ومع ذلك لم يدع الحسن والحسين (رضي الله عنهما) في الدفع عنه مقدوراً وكان أمر الله قدراً مقدوراً.
Among their objections against Uthman (may Allah be pleased with him) is that he appointed over the affairs of the Muslims those who showed corruption and depravity, such as al-Walid ibn 'Uqba, 'Abdullah ibn Abi Sarh, Marwan ibn al-Hakam, Mu'awiya ibn Abi Sufyan and those similar to them; and that he spent money from the treasury on his relatives until it is reported that he gave to four of them four hundred thousand dirhams; and that he reserved pasturage for himself. The Prophet (peace be upon him) said: 'There is no reserved pasturage except for Allah and His Messenger.' 'Umar only reserved pasturage for the weak Muslims and for camels of charity, jizya, and stray animals, not for himself. And that he burned the mushaf of Ibn Mas'ud, and beat him until he broke two of his ribs; and he beat 'Ammar until he developed a hernia; and he beat Abu Dharr and exiled him to al-Rabadha; and that he brought back al-Hakam ibn al-'As whom the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) had exiled; and that he dropped the blood-money from 'Abdullah ibn 'Umar after he killed al-Hurmuzan, and the prescribed punishment from al-Walid ibn 'Uqba after he drank wine; and that the Companions abandoned him until he was killed, and he was not buried except after three days.

The answer is that some of these matters do not harm his imamate, such as the appearance of corruption and depravity from the governors of some regions, for he had no knowledge of their inner states; he only had to act according to the apparent, and to dismiss them when corruption was confirmed. And Mu'awiya was over Syria in the time of 'Umar as well, and the school [of thought] is that the rebel is not a sinner. Even if it were granted, that appeared only during the imamate of 'Ali (may Allah be pleased with him). Some of them are pure fabrication, such as spending that amount from the treasury on his relatives, taking reserved pasturage for himself, and beating the Companions to the mentioned extent. Some of them are ijtihad matters delegated to the opinion of the imam according to what he sees as beneficial, such as discipline and discretionary punishment, and averting prescribed punishments and retaliation due to doubts and interpretations. Some of them were with the permission of the Prophet, like bringing back al-Hakam ibn al-'As, based on the report that he mentioned that to Abu Bakr and 'Umar (may Allah be pleased with them) and they said: 'You are a single witness.' So when the matter came to him, he ruled based on his knowledge. As for the report of the Companions abandoning him and leaving his burial without excuse, if it were true, it would be a defect in them, not in him. And we do not think that the Emigrants and Helpers (may Allah be pleased with them all), and especially 'Ali ibn Abi Talib (may Allah be pleased with him), that they would be pleased with the killing of their imam, oppressed in their land, and the leaving of a dead person unburied in their vicinity, especially one who is devout in the hours of the night, prostrating and standing, and constant throughout the day, remembering and fasting; the Messenger of Allah honored him by giving him his two daughters, gave him glad tidings of Paradise, and praised him. So how could they abandon him, when he was from their group, and had lived long in supporting them, and they knew his precedence in Islam and his ending in the abode of peace? But he did not permit them to fight, and he was not pleased with what they attempted of defense, out of caution against shedding blood, and satisfaction with the prior decree. Despite that, al-Hasan and al-Husayn (may Allah be pleased with them) did not leave any possible effort in defending him, and the command of Allah was a determined decree.
Source pages p. 285

Commentary
This passage is a detailed objection-and-answer. The author first enumerates several accusations against Caliph Uthman (may Allah be pleased with him): appointing corrupt governors, misusing treasury funds, reserving pasturage for himself, burning the mushaf of Ibn Mas'ud, beating companions, exiling Abu Dharr, returning al-Hakam ibn al-'As who was exiled by the Prophet, dropping prescribed punishments, and the abandonment by the Companions. The refutation categorizes the accusations into four groups: (1) Some do not harm his imamate, like appointing governors who appeared outwardly just but later became corrupt; the imam judges by the apparent and dismisses when corruption is proven. Mu'awiya was appointed by 'Umar as well, and the school holds that a rebel (bāghī) is not a sinner (fāsiq). (2) Some are pure fabrications, like the specific large sums to relatives, the reserved pasturage, and the severe beatings. (3) Some are matters of ijtihad left to the imam's judgment for discipline and averting punishments due to legal doubts. (4) Some were done with the Prophet's permission, like returning al-Hakam, as the Prophet had said that two witnesses are needed for exile but 'Uthman was a single witness. As for the abandonment, if true, it would reflect on the Companions, not Uthman; the author argues it is inconceivable that the best Companions, especially 'Ali, would abandon such a virtuous caliph, but they refrained from fighting to avoid bloodshed, and the two sons of 'Ali did all they could. The conclusion emphasizes divine decree.

Key Terms
الحكم بن العاص
خذلان
اجتهاد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the refutation, what was the basis for Uthman's decision to return al-Hakam ibn al-'As?
A) He ignored the Prophet's exile
B) He had permission from the Prophet based on a report he personally heard
C) He was following a dream
D) He was forced by relatives

Key Takeaway
Uthman's actions were either based on prophetic permission or defensible as part of his ijtihad, and the Companions did not abandon him but followed his directive to avoid bloodshed.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you are faced with a situation where defending the truth seems to require conflict, are you patient and trusting of Allah's decree, like 'Uthman, or do you rush to fight? What is holding you back from trusting Allah's plan in such moments?
?
If you were among the Companions, would you have obeyed 'Uthman's command not to fight, or would you have felt compelled to defend him despite his orders? What does your answer reveal about your obedience to legitimate authority in matters of public welfare?
?
How can you apply 'Uthman's example of forbidding well-intentioned defenders to prevent greater harm? Is there a situation in your life where you need to tell someone 'Do not defend me' for the sake of unity, even if it means personal sacrifice? How will you muster the courage to do so?

Classification of Accusations Against Ali
Sanusi categorizes accusations against Ali.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how scholars classify the accusations against Ali ibn Abi Talib into three categories: pure slander, matters of ijtihad, and actions permitted by the Prophet.

Core Idea
Not all accusations are equal—some are slander, some ijtihad, some permitted.

In defending Ali, scholars like Sanusi first clarify that the rebels who fought him are not considered sinners. Then they address the various criticisms by categorizing them into distinct types: pure slander, matters of ijtihad, and those permitted by the Prophet. This framework helps Muslims evaluate historical claims fairly.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice how Sanusi begins by stating that the rebel is not a sinner, then introduces the categories.
This sets the stage for the defense.
2
Identify the three categories
Look for the three distinct categories of accusations: pure slander, ijtihad, and actions with prophetic permission. Note the examples given for each.
The passage uses 'بعضها' to introduce each category.
3
Reflect on the importance of this classification
Ask yourself why such categorization is important. How does it prevent unfair judgment of historical figures? This connects to the learning objective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الباغي ليس بفاسق ولو سلم، فإنما ظهر ذلك في زمان إمامة علي (رضي الله عنه). وبعضها افتراء محض، كصرف ذلك القدر من بيت المال إلى أقاربه، وأخذ الحمى لنفسه، وضرب الصحابة إلى الحد المذكور. وبعضها اجتهاديات مفوضة إلى رأي الإمام حسب ما يراه من المصلحة كالتأديب والتعزير، ودرء الحدود والقصاص بالشبهات والتأويلات. وبعضها كان بإذن النبي كرد الحكم بن العاص على ما روي أنه ذكر ذلك لأبي بكر وعمر (رضي الله عنهما) فقالا : إنك شاهد واحد. فلما آل الأمر إليه حكم بعلمه. وأما حديث خذلان الصحابة إياه وتركهم دفنه من غير عذر، فلو صح، كان قدحاً فيهم لا فيه. ونحن لا نظن بالمهاجرين والأنصار (رضي الله عنهم) عموماً، وبعلي بن أبي طالب (رضي الله عنه) خصوصاً أن يرضوا بقتل مظلوم في دارهم وترك دفن ميت في جوارهم، سيما من هو قانت آناء الليل ساجداً وقائماً، وعاكف طول النهار، ذاكراً وصائماً، شرفه رسول الله بابنتيه، وبشره بالجنة، وأثنى عليه. فكيف يخذلونه، وقد كان من زمرتهم، وطول العمر في نصرتهم، وعلموا سابقته في الإسلام وخاتمته إلى دار السلام. لكنه لم يأذن لهم في المحاربة، ولم يرض بما حاولوا من المدافعة، تحامياً عن إراقة الدماء، ورضاً بسابق القضاء. ومع ذلك لم يدع الحسن والحسين (رضي الله عنهما) في الدفع عنه مقدوراً وكان أمر الله قدراً مقدوراً.
The rebel is not a sinner, even if he surrenders. That only appeared during the time of the Imamate of Ali (may Allah be pleased with him). Some of the accusations are pure slander, such as diverting that amount from the treasury to his relatives, taking the reserved pasture for himself, and beating the Companions to the mentioned extent. Some are matters of ijtihad delegated to the opinion of the Imam according to what he sees as beneficial, like discipline and discretionary punishment, and averting prescribed punishments and retaliation due to doubts and interpretations. Some were with the permission of the Prophet, like the return of al-Hakam ibn al-‘As according to what is narrated: that he mentioned that to Abu Bakr and ‘Umar (may Allah be pleased with them) and they said, 'You are a single witness.' When the matter came to him, he judged based on his knowledge. As for the hadith about the Companions abandoning him and leaving his burial without excuse, if it were authentic, it would be a defect in them, not in him. And we do not think of the Muhajirun and Ansar (may Allah be pleased with them all) generally, and of ‘Ali ibn Abi Talib (may Allah be pleased with him) specifically, that they would consent to the killing of an oppressed person in their homes and leave the burial of a dead person in their neighborhood, especially one who is devout during the hours of the night, prostrating and standing, and devoted during the day, remembering and fasting, whom the Messenger of Allah honored with his two daughters, gave glad tidings of Paradise, and praised. How could they abandon him, when he was from their group, spent his long life in supporting them, and they knew his precedence in Islam and his end in the Abode of Peace? But he did not permit them to fight, nor was he pleased with what they attempted of defense, out of caution against bloodshed, and satisfaction with the prior decree. Despite that, he did not leave any capability for al-Hasan and al-Husayn (may Allah be pleased with them) to defend him, and the command of Allah was a decreed decree.
Source pages p. 286

Commentary
This passage defends ‘Ali against various accusations by categorizing them. First, it states that the rebel (i.e., those who fought ‘Ali) are not considered sinners, even if they surrendered—this appeared only during ‘Ali’s caliphate. Then it classifies the accusations: some are pure slander (e.g., misappropriating treasury funds, taking reserved land, beating Companions); some are matters of ijtihad delegated to the Imam’s discretion (e.g., discipline, averting punishments due to doubts); some were permitted by the Prophet himself (e.g., the case of al-Hakam ibn al-‘As). Next, it refutes the claim that the Companions abandoned ‘Ali and left him unburied. If this hadith were true, it would reflect badly on them, not on ‘Ali. The author argues that the Muhajirun and Ansar, and especially ‘Ali, would never condone such acts. ‘Ali was highly honored by the Prophet, so how could they abandon him? The reason ‘Ali did not fight back was to avoid bloodshed, accepting divine decree. Even al-Hasan and al-Husayn were unable to defend him, and Allah’s command is predetermined.

Key Terms
باغٍ
فاسق
افتراء
اجتهاد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All accusations against Ali are true and indicate wrongdoing.
Correction: The passage shows that many accusations are slander, others are legitimate ijtihad, and some were even permitted by the Prophet himself.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the status of the rebel (bāghī) who fought Ali?
A) He is a sinner without exception
B) He is not a sinner even if he surrenders
C) He is a sinner only if he does not repent
D) His status depends on the imam's judgment

Key Takeaway
The accusations against Ali fall into three categories: slander, ijtihad, and prophetic permission — not all are valid criticisms.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hear criticisms against a righteous person, do you immediately assume they are true, or do you seek to classify them as this lesson teaches? What accusation have you recently believed without verifying?
?
In your own life, when you make a decision based on your best judgment (ijtihad), do you accept that others may disagree without considering you a sinner? How do you react when someone calls your judgment into question?
?
Ali endured baseless slander even after the Prophet's permission. How do you handle false accusations against you? Do you rely on Allah and the truth, or do you become defensive and angry? What would it take for you to respond with the same dignity?

Refuting the Claim that Companions Abandoned Ali
Sanusi refutes the hadith of abandonment and explains Ali's stance.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the defense against the claim that the Companions abandoned Ali and left his burial, and why Ali chose not to fight back.

Core Idea
The Companions did not abandon Ali; he chose peace to avoid bloodshed.

One of the most serious accusations is that the Companions abandoned Ali during his caliphate and neglected his burial. This passage refutes that claim by pointing out that if such a hadith were true, it would reflect badly on the Companions, not on Ali. Moreover, the character of the Muhajirun and Ansar, and especially Ali, contradicts such behavior. The passage explains that Ali refrained from fighting to prevent bloodshed, accepting divine decree.

Guided Path
1
Read the refutation of the abandonment claim
Read the Arabic passage dealing with the hadith about abandonment. Notice how the author argues that if true, it would discredit the Companions, not Ali.
This is a logical argument.
2
Analyze the reasoning
Reflect on why the author says the Companions could not have abandoned Ali, given their noble character and Ali's virtues. Also consider why Ali did not permit them to fight.
The author uses a 'how could they?' rhetorical style.
3
Understand the theological stance
Note that Ali's acceptance of divine decree and his caution against bloodshed explain his peaceful stance.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Companions abandoned Ali and left his burial out of negligence or hostility.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why did Ali not permit the Companions to fight in his defense?
A) He was too weak to command them
B) He wanted to avoid bloodshed and accept the divine decree
C) He was angry with them
D) He thought they would fail

Key Takeaway
The claim that Companions abandoned Ali is refuted by their noble character and by Ali's own choice to avoid conflict.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you are wronged or misunderstood, do you rush to defend yourself and prove others wrong, or are you willing to remain silent and trust Allah's decree, as Ali did? What would it cost you to choose peace over vindication?
?
This passage gives the Companions the benefit of the doubt, assuming the best of them even when faced with a troubling narration. In your heart, when you hear stories that cast doubt on the early Muslims, do you instinctively defend them or suspect them? What does that reveal about your own reliance on Hasan az-Zann (good opinion)?
?
Ali sacrificed his right to be defended and buried properly for the sake of unity and avoiding bloodshed. Is there a conflict in your life (family, community, or even online) where you are holding onto your 'right to retaliate'? Will you surrender that right today for the sake of Allah, even if it means being misunderstood by others?

The Shura After Umar and the Selection of Uthman
Narrates the shura after Umar's death and the selection of Uthman.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the historical process of the shura after Umar's death and the doctrinal significance of Ali's response regarding ijtihad.

Core Idea
Ali's statement on ijtihad was a legal principle, not a rejection of the earlier caliphs.

This passage from a kalam work recounts the shura (consultation) that led to Uthman's election as caliph after Umar's death. It also clarifies a potentially misunderstood statement by Ali, explaining that his insistence on exercising personal judgment (ijtihad) was a matter of legal methodology, not a challenge to the legitimacy of Abu Bakr and Umar. Understanding this helps resolve sectarian disputes over early Islamic leadership.

Guided Path
1
Read the narrative of the shura
First, read the Arabic passage and its translation to grasp the sequence of events.
Pay attention to the key figures and their roles.
2
Note the delegations of votes
Identify who delegated their vote to whom, and how the final choice was made.
Al-Zubayr to Ali, Talhah to Uthman, Sa'd to Abd al-Rahman.
3
Focus on Ali's response to Abd al-Rahman
When asked to pledge on the sira of the two shaykhs, Ali agreed to the Book and Sunnah but added 'I will exert my own judgment.'
This is the key moment for doctrinal discussion.
4
Understand the doctrinal clarification
Read the commentary that explains Ali's statement was about ijtihad, not rejection of the caliphs.
The text explicitly states it is not opposition to the imamate of Abu Bakr and Umar.
5
Reflect on the legal disagreement
Consider the difference in views between Ali and Uthman/Abd al-Rahman regarding taqlid.
Ali held a mujtahid should not imitate, while Uthman held it permissible if the other is more knowledgeable.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
للمغيرة بن شعبة طعنه وهو في الصلاة، وحين علم بالموت قال : ما أجد أحداً أحق بهذا الأمر من هؤلاء النفر الذين توفي رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم وهو عنهم راض فسمى علياً ، وعثمان، والزبير، وطلحة ، وعبد الرحمن بن عوف، وسعد بن أبي وقاص . وجعل الخلافة شورى بينهم ، فاجتمعوا بعد دفن عمر (رضي الله عنه) فقال الزبير : قد جعلت أمري إلى علي. وقال طلحة : قد جعلت أمري إلى عثمان . وقال سعد: قد جعلت أمري الى عبد الرحمن بن عوف. ثم جعلوا الاختيار إلى عبد الرحمن بن عوف ، فأخذ بيد علي رضي الله تعالى عنه وقال : تبايعني على كتاب الله وسنة رسول الله وسيرة الشيخين . فقال : على كتاب الله وسنة رسول الله ، وأجتهد برأيي . ثم قال مثل ذلك لعثمان فأجابه إلى ما دعاه ، وكرر عليهما ثلاث مرات ، فأجابا بالجواب الأول، فبايع عثمان وبايعه الناس ، ورضوا بإمامته. وقول علي رضي الله تعالى عنه : «وأجتهد برأيي» ليس خلافاً منه في إمامة الشيخين بل ذهاباً إلى أنه لا يجوز للمجتهد تقليد مجتهد آخر بل عليه اتباع اجتهاده، وكان من مذهب عثمان وعبد الرحمن أنه يجوز إذا كان الآخر أعلم وأبصر بوجوه المقاييس.
Al-Mughirah ibn Shu'bah stabbed him while he was in prayer, and when he learned of death, he said: 'I do not find anyone more entitled to this matter than these men with whom the Messenger of God, peace be upon him, passed away while he was pleased with them.' So he named 'Ali, 'Uthman, al-Zubayr, Talhah, 'Abd al-Rahman ibn 'Awf, and Sa'd ibn Abi Waqqas. And he made the caliphate a consultation (shura) among them. So they gathered after the burial of 'Umar (may God be pleased with him), and al-Zubayr said: 'I have entrusted my affair to 'Ali.' Talhah said: 'I have entrusted my affair to 'Uthman.' Sa'd said: 'I have entrusted my affair to 'Abd al-Rahman ibn 'Awf.' Then they delegated the choice to 'Abd al-Rahman ibn 'Awf. He took the hand of 'Ali (may God be pleased with him) and said: 'Will you pledge allegiance to me on the Book of God, the Sunnah of His Messenger, and the conduct (sirah) of the two shaykhs?' He said: 'On the Book of God and the Sunnah of His Messenger, and I will exert my own judgment (ijtihad).' Then he said the same to 'Uthman, and he responded to what he called him to. He repeated it to them three times, and they both gave the first answer, so he pledged allegiance to 'Uthman, and the people pledged allegiance to him, and they were satisfied with his imamate. And the statement of 'Ali (may God be pleased with him): 'And I will exert my own judgment' is not opposition from him to the imamate of the two shaykhs, but rather a view that it is not permissible for a mujtahid to imitate (taqlid) another mujtahid, but rather he must follow his own ijtihad. And it was the school (madhhab) of 'Uthman and 'Abd al-Rahman that it is permissible if the other is more knowledgeable and more discerning in the ways of analogies (qiyas).
Source pages p. 288

Commentary
This is a historical narrative passage from a kalam work on imamate, describing the shura (consultation) after 'Umar's death and the selection of 'Uthman as caliph. The passage begins by recounting that al-Mughirah ibn Shu'bah stabbed 'Umar while he was in prayer, and when 'Umar realized he was dying, he named six companions as candidates for the caliphate, stating that the Prophet died pleased with them. He then made the caliphate a shura among them. After 'Umar's burial, the six gathered, and al-Zubayr delegated his vote to 'Ali, Talhah delegated his to 'Uthman, and Sa'd delegated his to 'Abd al-Rahman ibn 'Awf. The others then entrusted the final choice to 'Abd al-Rahman. 'Abd al-Rahman first approached 'Ali and asked him to pledge on the Book, Sunnah, and the conduct of Abu Bakr and 'Umar. 'Ali agreed to the Book and Sunnah but added 'and I will exert my own judgment (ijtihad).' When 'Abd al-Rahman asked the same of 'Uthman, he accepted fully. After repeating the question three times with the same responses, 'Abd al-Rahman pledged allegiance to 'Uthman, and the people followed. The passage then explains that 'Ali's statement about ijtihad was not a rejection of the legitimacy of the first two caliphs, but rather a legal principle: a mujtahid should not blindly imitate another mujtahid but must follow his own reasoning. It notes that 'Uthman and 'Abd al-Rahman held the opposite view—that it is permissible to imitate a more knowledgeable scholar. Thus, the narrative defends the legitimacy of 'Uthman's election and clarifies 'Ali's position.

Key Terms
الشورى
الاجتهاد
الإمامة
التقليد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Ali's refusal to pledge on the sira of the two shaykhs meant he rejected their caliphate.
Correction: Ali's statement about ijtihad was a legal principle; he held that a mujtahid should not imitate another mujtahid but follow his own reasoning.

Checkpoint
Why did 'Ali respond with 'And I will exert my own judgment' when asked to pledge on the conduct of the two shaykhs?
A) He rejected the legitimacy of Abu Bakr and 'Umar's caliphate.
B) He held that a mujtahid should not imitate another mujtahid but follow his own ijtihad.
C) He did not want to pledge allegiance to 'Uthman.
D) He was unsure of the correct answer.

Key Takeaway
The shura process was a legitimate consultation, and Ali's response was a principled stance on ijtihad, not opposition to the earlier caliphs.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you see a fellow Muslim disagreeing on a religious practice, do you immediately assume they are rejecting the core beliefs, or do you pause to consider their methodological principles—like 'Ali's ijtihad stance? Which approach will you adopt starting today?
?
'Ali held firmly to his principle of ijtihad even though it cost him the immediate caliphate. Is there a principle you hold onto so strongly that you would accept a personal loss rather than compromise it? If not, why?
?
The author clarifies that differences in methodology do not imply opposition to authority. Think of a recent disagreement you had with someone in your community: did you attribute bad intentions to them? How can you apply this lesson's insight to that relationship?

The Rebellion Against Uthman and the Legitimacy of Ali's Caliphate
Describes the rebellion against Uthman and Ali's subsequent caliphate.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author defends the legitimacy of Ali's caliphate by portraying the rebels against Uthman as disreputable and emphasizing the broad support from senior Companions.

Core Idea
The rebellion against Uthman was led by riffraff; Ali's caliphate was legitimate.

This passage addresses a critical moment in early Islamic history: the rebellion against Caliph Uthman and the subsequent election of Ali. The author argues that the rebels were not reputable Companions but scum, and that Ali accepted the caliphate reluctantly with the support of many senior Companions. This narrative defends the legitimacy of Ali's leadership and absolves the community of blame.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Let the historical narrative and its argument unfold.
Notice the strong language used to describe the rebels.
2
Identify the key groups mentioned
Identify the rebels (الرعاع والأوباش) and the senior Companions (أكابر الصحابة, أهل الشورى, المبشرون بالجنة). Ask: Why does the author distinguish them sharply?
This distinction is central to the argument.
3
Examine the list of those who pledged to Ali
Notice the names: Khuzaymah ibn Thabit, Abu al-Haytham, Muhammad ibn Muslim, 'Ammar, Ibn 'Abbas, Talhah, al-Zubayr, 'Abdullah ibn 'Umar, Sa'd ibn Abi Waqqas, Abu Musa, Muhammad ibn Maslamah. What does this list convey about the legitimacy of Ali's caliphate?
Some later opposed him but repented; others excused themselves from fighting.
4
Reflect on the exception of some Companions
Consider why some Companions asked to be excused from fighting. How does this fit into the narrative? Does it undermine legitimacy or show piety?
The author uses hadiths to explain their stance.
5
Synthesise the argument
Summarise how the passage defends Ali's caliphate: the rebels were disreputable, Uthman's killing was unjust, and Ali had broad support from the best of the Companions.
This counters sectarian claims that Ali's caliphate was disputed or illegitimate.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم خرج على عثمان بعد اثنتي عشرة سنة من خلافته رعاع وأوباش من كل أوب، وأرذال من خزاعة، ليس فيهم أحد من كبار الصحابة وأهل العلم ومن يعتد به من أوساط الناس. فقتلوه ظلماً وعدواناً في ذي الحجة سنة خمس وثلاثين. ولو استحق القتل أو الخلع لما ترك أكابر الصحابة ومن بقي من أهل الشورى، ومن المبشرين بالجنة ذلك إلى جمع من الأوباش والأرذال ومن لا سابقة له في الإسلام، ولا علم بشيء من أمور الدين ثم اجتمع الناس بعد ثلاثة ايام على علي (رضي الله تعالى عنه) والتمسوا منه القيام بأمر الخلافة لكونه أولى الناس بذلك. وأفضلهم في ذلك الزمان ، فقبله بعد امتناع كثير ومدافعة طويلة، وبايعه جماعة ممن حضر كخزيمة بن ثابت وأبي الهيثم بن التيهان ومحمد بن مسلم، وعمار، الأشعري، وعبد الله بن عباس وغيرهم. وكذا طلحة والزبير، وقد صحت توبتهما عن مخالفته، وكذا بايعه عبد الله بن عمر ، وسعد بن أبي وقاص، وأبي موسى ومحمد بن مسلمة، إلا أنهم استعفوا عن القتال مع أهل القبلة لما رووا في هذا المعنى من الأحاديث.
Then there rose against 'Uthman after twelve years of his caliphate the rabble and riffraff from every direction, and the scum of Khuza'ah, among whom there was not one of the senior companions, nor of the people of knowledge, nor of those considered among the middle class of people. So they killed him wrongfully and aggressively in Dhu al-Hijjah of the year thirty-five. Had he deserved killing or deposition, the senior companions, those remaining from the people of the shura, and those given glad tidings of Paradise would not have left that to a collection of riffraff and scum who have no precedence in Islam nor any knowledge of the affairs of religion. Then the people gathered after three days to 'Ali (may God be pleased with him) and asked him to undertake the matter of the caliphate because he was the most entitled of people to that, and the most virtuous among them at that time. So he accepted after much refusal and prolonged resistance, and a group of those present pledged allegiance to him, such as Khuzaymah ibn Thabit, Abu al-Haytham ibn al-Tayyihan, Muhammad ibn Muslim, 'Ammar al-Ash'ari, 'Abdullah ibn 'Abbas, and others. Likewise, Talhah and al-Zubayr, and their repentance from opposing him was confirmed. Likewise, 'Abdullah ibn 'Umar, Sa'd ibn Abi Waqqas, Abu Musa, and Muhammad ibn Maslamah pledged allegiance to him, except that they sought to be excused from fighting the people of the qiblah due to the hadiths they had narrated on this meaning.
Source pages p. 288

Commentary
This passage continues the historical narrative on imamate, focusing on the rebellion against 'Uthman and the subsequent caliphate of 'Ali. The author describes those who revolted against 'Uthman after twelve years as 'rabble and riffraff' from various places, specifically the scum of Khuza'ah, emphasizing that none of the senior companions, scholars, or reputable middle-class people were among them. They killed 'Uthman unjustly in Dhu al-Hijjah 35 AH. The passage argues that if 'Uthman had deserved death or removal, the senior companions, the remaining members of the shura, and those promised Paradise would not have left that task to such disreputable people. Then, three days after 'Uthman's death, the people gathered around 'Ali and requested him to take the caliphate because he was the most entitled and most virtuous at that time. 'Ali accepted after much reluctance and hesitation. A group of companions pledged allegiance to him, including Khuzaymah ibn Thabit, Abu al-Haytham, Muhammad ibn Muslim, 'Ammar al-Ash'ari, 'Abdullah ibn 'Abbas, and others. Talhah and al-Zubayr also pledged, and their repentance from opposing 'Ali was confirmed. 'Abdullah ibn 'Umar, Sa'd ibn Abi Waqqas, Abu Musa, and Muhammad ibn Maslamah also pledged allegiance, but they asked to be excused from fighting fellow Muslims due to hadiths they had heard about avoiding inter-Muslim warfare. The passage defends 'Ali's caliphate by showing it was based on broad acceptance from key companions, while the rebels against 'Uthman were disreputable, thus absolving the community of blame.

Key Terms
الرعاع
الأوباش
الصحابة
البيعة
الإمامة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The rebellion against 'Uthman was led by respected Companions or was justified by the community.
Correction: The passage insists the rebels were disreputable rabble with no standing, and that senior Companions did not participate or approve.

Checkpoint
Why does the author emphasize that the rebels against 'Uthman were riffraff and not senior companions?
A) To show that 'Uthman was unjustly killed.
B) To argue that the caliphate was not legitimate after him.
C) To prove that 'Ali's caliphate was also illegitimate.
D) To demonstrate that the companions were divided.

Key Takeaway
The caliphate of Ali was legitimate, accepted by the majority of Companions, and the rebellion against Uthman was the work of disreputable elements.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hear criticism of a leader or scholar, do you first investigate the character of the accusers? The rebels against 'Uthman had no standing in Islam—yet they succeeded in swaying opinion. Where in your life have you been swayed by voices lacking credibility? Will you commit to verifying the source of every accusation before forming a judgment?
?
The senior companions like 'Abdullah ibn 'Umar pledged allegiance to Ali but refrained from fighting fellow Muslims, citing hadiths. Is there a duty you are avoiding by using a religious excuse? How can you ensure that your hesitation is genuine piety, not a convenient escape from responsibility? Choose one such area and decide on a concrete action this week.
?
Ali accepted the caliphate only after much refusal and prolonged resistance. Have you ever taken on a responsibility reluctantly? What does his example teach you about accepting leadership when the community calls you, even if it is difficult? Reflect on one role you currently avoid and ask yourself: is it humility or fear holding you back?

Evidence for Ali's Caliphate in Sunni Sources
Presents hadith and consensus evidence for Ali's caliphate.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Sunni arguments for the legitimacy of Ali's caliphate based on prophetic hadith and consensus.

Core Idea
Ali's caliphate was established by consensus and prophetic hadith.

This passage presents the classical Sunni case for Ali's caliphate, citing three key hadiths and an argument from the consensus of the shura companions. It also addresses objections from those who deny consensus on his imamate.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to grasp the flow. Notice how the author first asserts that Ali's caliphate was established by bay'a and agreement.
2
Identify the three hadith proofs
Find the three hadiths cited: the thirty-year caliphate, the prophecy about fighting three groups, and the hadith about Ammar. Consider how each supports Ali's caliphate.
The third hadith is implicitly linked because Ammar was killed under Ali's banner at Siffin.
3
Understand the shura consensus argument
The author argues that the shura consensus that the caliphate was either for Uthman or Ali implies that after Uthman's death, it belongs to Ali. Follow this logical step.
4
Examine Imam al-Haramayn's rebuttal
Read the quote from Imam al-Haramayn (al-Juwayni) dismissing the claim that there was no consensus on Ali's imamate. Note his distinction between denying the imamate and the later fitnas.
This addresses a potential objection from those who say there was no consensus.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبالجملة انعقدت خلافته بالبيعة واتفاق أهل الحل والعقد . وقد دلت عليه أحاديث كقوله (عليه السلام) الخلافة بعدي ثلاثون سنة وقوله (عليه السلام) لعلي (رضي الله تعالى عنه): إنك تقاتل الناكثين والمارقين والقاسطين. وقوله (عليه السلام) لعمار: تقتلك الفئة الباغية . وقد قتل يوم صفين تحت راية علي رضي الله تعالى عنه . ومن المتكلمين من يدعي الإجماع على خلافته لأنه انعقد لإجماع زمان الشورى. على أن الخلافة لعثمان أو علي. وهو إجماع على أنه لولا عثمان فهي لعلي فحين خرج عثمان من البين بالقتل، بقي لعلي بالإجماع . قال إمام الحرمين : لا اكتراث بقول من قال : لا إجماع على إمامة علي رضي الله تعالى عنه فإن الإمامة لم تجحد له، وإنما هاجت الفتن لأمور أخر. قال : وأما الشيعة فيزعمون أن الإمام بعد النبي علي كرم الله وجهه ثم الحسن، ثم الحسين ثم علي زين العابدين، ثم محمد الباقر، ثم جعفر الصادق، ثم موسى الكاظم ، ثم علي الرضا، ثم محمد الجواد، ثم علي الزكي، ثم الحسن العسكري، ثم محمد المنتظر المهدي وأنه تواتر نص كل على من بعده وأن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم قال للحسين : ابني هذا إمام ابن إمام أخو إمام أبو أئمة تسعة، تاسعهم قائمهم. ونحن لا نزيد على التعجب). يعني أن الإمامية يزعمون أن الإمام الحق بعد رسول الله (صلى الله عليه وسلم) علي ، ثم ابنه
In summary, his caliphate was established by the pledge of allegiance and the agreement of the people of solution and contract. And hadiths indicate it, such as his saying (peace be upon him): 'The caliphate after me will be thirty years,' and his saying (peace be upon him) to 'Ali (may God be pleased with him): 'You will fight the breakers of the oath (al-nakithin), the deviators (al-mariqin), and the unjust (al-qasitin),' and his saying (peace be upon him) to 'Ammar: 'The rebellious faction will kill you.' And he was killed on the day of Siffin under the banner of 'Ali (may God be pleased with him). And among the theologians (mutakallimun) are those who claim consensus (ijma) on his caliphate because the consensus of the time of the shura was established that the caliphate is either for 'Uthman or 'Ali. It is a consensus that if not for 'Uthman, it would be for 'Ali. So when 'Uthman was removed from the picture by killing, it remained for 'Ali by consensus. Imam al-Haramayn said: 'No heed is given to the saying of one who says there is no consensus on the imamate of 'Ali (may God be pleased with him), for the imamate was not denied to him; rather, the seditions arose for other reasons.' He said: 'As for the Shi'a, they claim that the Imam after the Prophet is 'Ali (may God honor his face), then al-Hasan, then al-Husayn, then 'Ali Zayn al-'Abidin, then Muhammad al-Baqir, then Ja'far al-Sadiq, then Musa al-Kazim, then 'Ali al-Rida, then Muhammad al-Jawad, then 'Ali al-Zaki, then al-Hasan al-'Askari, then Muhammad al-Muntazar al-Mahdi, and that there is a recurrent transmission (tawatur) of a text (nass) from each to the one after him, and that the Prophet (peace be upon him) said to al-Husayn: 'This son of mine is an imam, son of an imam, brother of an imam, father of nine imams, the ninth of whom is their Qa'im.' And we can add nothing but astonishment.' That is, the Imamiyya claim that the true Imam after the Messenger of God (peace be upon him) is 'Ali, then his son...
Source pages p. 289

Commentary
This passage is a conclusion and a double apologetic argument: it establishes the legitimacy of 'Ali's caliphate through textual evidence and consensus (ijma), and then refutes the Imami Shi'a doctrine of imamate. The author begins by stating that 'Ali's caliphate was established by bay'a and agreement of the ahl al-hall wa al-'aqd (those who loosen and bind). He then cites three hadiths as evidence: the prophecy that the caliphate after the Prophet would last thirty years (which includes 'Ali's rule), the Prophet's prediction that 'Ali would fight three groups (the oath-breakers at the Battle of the Camel, the deviators at Siffin, and the unjust from Kharijites), and the hadith that 'Ammar would be killed by the rebellious faction, which occurred at Siffin under 'Ali's banner. The author then presents an argument from the theologians: the consensus of the shura companions that the caliphate would go either to 'Uthman or 'Ali implies that when 'Uthman died, the position automatically fell to 'Ali by consensus. He quotes Imam al-Haramayn (al-Juwayni) dismissing the claim that there was no consensus on 'Ali's imamate, arguing that the imamate was never denied to him; the later fitnas had other causes. Finally, the author briefly dismisses the Imami Shi'a list of twelve imams from 'Ali to Muhammad al-Mahdi, noting their claim of tawatur of nass (recurrent transmission of explicit designation) and the purported hadith about nine imams from al-Husayn's lineage, remarking with astonishment. The final fragment 'that is, the Imamiyya claim that the true Imam after the Messenger of God is 'Ali, then his son' likely continues the refutation. The passage defends Sunni orthodoxy on the caliphate while rejecting Shi'a imamate theology.

Key Terms
البيعة
أهل الحل والعقد
الإجماع
الشورى

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some claim there was no consensus on 'Ali's imamate.
Correction: Imam al-Haramayn argues that the imamate was never denied to 'Ali; the later fitnas had other causes.

Checkpoint
What is the 'consensus of the time of the shura' that the author uses to argue for 'Ali's caliphate?
A) Everyone agreed that 'Ali was the best companion.
B) The shura council decided that the caliphate is either for 'Uthman or 'Ali, so after 'Uthman's death, it goes to 'Ali.
C) All companions unanimously elected 'Ali after 'Uthman.
D) The Prophet designated 'Ali as his successor.

Key Takeaway
Ali's caliphate is legitimate through both explicit prophetic hadiths and the implicit consensus of the shura companions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In the shura, the companions narrowed the caliphate to Uthman or Ali, trusting the collective wisdom of the community. When you face a decision where the consensus of trustworthy people points in a direction you dislike, do you submit your preference to that collective judgment, or do you insist on your own view? What holds you back from trusting the community's discernment in matters of leadership?
?
The hadiths in this passage identify Ali's side as the party of truth, even though many righteous companions opposed him. In your own life, when you take a stand for what you believe is true, are you willing to accept that even good people may oppose you? How do you respond when those you respect disagree with your conviction—do you doubt yourself, or do you hold firm with humility?
?
The author dismisses objections to Ali's imamate by saying the seditions arose for other reasons. Think about a conflict you have observed or participated in: did you assume the other party's claims were illegitimate, or did you look for deeper causes? This lesson challenges you to examine the narratives you accept about authority and conflict. Are you ready to reconsider a situation where you blamed someone's lack of legitimacy, and instead ask what other factors were at play?

Refutation of Imami Shi'a Imamate Doctrine
Dismisses the Imami Shi'a succession of twelve imams.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Sunni response to the Imami Shi'a claim of twelve designated imams.

Core Idea
The Shi'a list of twelve imams lacks tawatur and is met with scholarly astonishment.

The author briefly presents the Imami Shi'a doctrine of twelver imams and their claim of recurrent transmission of explicit designation, then dismisses it with a note of astonishment. This section shows the Sunni stance on the issue.

Guided Path
1
Read the Shi'a list of imams
Read the passage listing the twelve imams from 'Ali to Muhammad al-Mahdi. Notice the claim of tawatur (recurrent transmission) of a text (nass) from each imam to the next.
2
Identify the hadith cited
The Shi'a claim a hadith where the Prophet tells al-Husayn about nine imams from his lineage. Identify the specific wording.
3
Understand the author's response
The author says 'We can add nothing but astonishment' – this indicates dismissal without detailed refutation. Consider why a Sunni scholar would react this way.
4
Reflect on the doctrinal point
Consider that Sunni tradition accepts the first four caliphs and does not recognize a designated line of imams after them. This passage highlights the disagreement.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبالجملة انعقدت خلافته بالبيعة واتفاق أهل الحل والعقد . وقد دلت عليه أحاديث كقوله (عليه السلام) الخلافة بعدي ثلاثون سنة وقوله (عليه السلام) لعلي (رضي الله تعالى عنه): إنك تقاتل الناكثين والمارقين والقاسطين. وقوله (عليه السلام) لعمار: تقتلك الفئة الباغية . وقد قتل يوم صفين تحت راية علي رضي الله تعالى عنه . ومن المتكلمين من يدعي الإجماع على خلافته لأنه انعقد لإجماع زمان الشورى. على أن الخلافة لعثمان أو علي. وهو إجماع على أنه لولا عثمان فهي لعلي فحين خرج عثمان من البين بالقتل، بقي لعلي بالإجماع . قال إمام الحرمين : لا اكتراث بقول من قال : لا إجماع على إمامة علي رضي الله تعالى عنه فإن الإمامة لم تجحد له، وإنما هاجت الفتن لأمور أخر. قال : وأما الشيعة فيزعمون أن الإمام بعد النبي علي كرم الله وجهه ثم الحسن، ثم الحسين ثم علي زين العابدين، ثم محمد الباقر، ثم جعفر الصادق، ثم موسى الكاظم ، ثم علي الرضا، ثم محمد الجواد، ثم علي الزكي، ثم الحسن العسكري، ثم محمد المنتظر المهدي وأنه تواتر نص كل على من بعده وأن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم قال للحسين : ابني هذا إمام ابن إمام أخو إمام أبو أئمة تسعة، تاسعهم قائمهم. ونحن لا نزيد على التعجب). يعني أن الإمامية يزعمون أن الإمام الحق بعد رسول الله (صلى الله عليه وسلم) علي ، ثم ابنه
In summary, his caliphate was established by the pledge of allegiance and the agreement of the people of solution and contract. And hadiths indicate it, such as his saying (peace be upon him): 'The caliphate after me will be thirty years,' and his saying (peace be upon him) to 'Ali (may God be pleased with him): 'You will fight the breakers of the oath (al-nakithin), the deviators (al-mariqin), and the unjust (al-qasitin),' and his saying (peace be upon him) to 'Ammar: 'The rebellious faction will kill you.' And he was killed on the day of Siffin under the banner of 'Ali (may God be pleased with him). And among the theologians (mutakallimun) are those who claim consensus (ijma) on his caliphate because the consensus of the time of the shura was established that the caliphate is either for 'Uthman or 'Ali. It is a consensus that if not for 'Uthman, it would be for 'Ali. So when 'Uthman was removed from the picture by killing, it remained for 'Ali by consensus. Imam al-Haramayn said: 'No heed is given to the saying of one who says there is no consensus on the imamate of 'Ali (may God be pleased with him), for the imamate was not denied to him; rather, the seditions arose for other reasons.' He said: 'As for the Shi'a, they claim that the Imam after the Prophet is 'Ali (may God honor his face), then al-Hasan, then al-Husayn, then 'Ali Zayn al-'Abidin, then Muhammad al-Baqir, then Ja'far al-Sadiq, then Musa al-Kazim, then 'Ali al-Rida, then Muhammad al-Jawad, then 'Ali al-Zaki, then al-Hasan al-'Askari, then Muhammad al-Muntazar al-Mahdi, and that there is a recurrent transmission (tawatur) of a text (nass) from each to the one after him, and that the Prophet (peace be upon him) said to al-Husayn: 'This son of mine is an imam, son of an imam, brother of an imam, father of nine imams, the ninth of whom is their Qa'im.' And we can add nothing but astonishment.' That is, the Imamiyya claim that the true Imam after the Messenger of God (peace be upon him) is 'Ali, then his son...
Source pages p. 289

Commentary
This passage is a conclusion and a double apologetic argument: it establishes the legitimacy of 'Ali's caliphate through textual evidence and consensus (ijma), and then refutes the Imami Shi'a doctrine of imamate. The author begins by stating that 'Ali's caliphate was established by bay'a and agreement of the ahl al-hall wa al-'aqd (those who loosen and bind). He then cites three hadiths as evidence: the prophecy that the caliphate after the Prophet would last thirty years (which includes 'Ali's rule), the Prophet's prediction that 'Ali would fight three groups (the oath-breakers at the Battle of the Camel, the deviators at Siffin, and the unjust from Kharijites), and the hadith that 'Ammar would be killed by the rebellious faction, which occurred at Siffin under 'Ali's banner. The author then presents an argument from the theologians: the consensus of the shura companions that the caliphate would go either to 'Uthman or 'Ali implies that when 'Uthman died, the position automatically fell to 'Ali by consensus. He quotes Imam al-Haramayn (al-Juwayni) dismissing the claim that there was no consensus on 'Ali's imamate, arguing that the imamate was never denied to him; the later fitnas had other causes. Finally, the author briefly dismisses the Imami Shi'a list of twelve imams from 'Ali to Muhammad al-Mahdi, noting their claim of tawatur of nass (recurrent transmission of explicit designation) and the purported hadith about nine imams from al-Husayn's lineage, remarking with astonishment. The final fragment 'that is, the Imamiyya claim that the true Imam after the Messenger of God is 'Ali, then his son' likely continues the refutation. The passage defends Sunni orthodoxy on the caliphate while rejecting Shi'a imamate theology.

التواتر

الإمامية
الشيعة

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think the Shi'a imamate doctrine is supported by authentic prophetic hadith in Sunni sources.
Correction: The author treats the Shi'a hadith with astonishment, indicating it is not accepted in Sunni tradition.

Checkpoint
What is the author's response to the Shi'a claim of twelve imams?
A) He accepts it based on tawatur.
B) He refutes it with detailed arguments.
C) He expresses astonishment and dismisses it.
D) He refutes it using the hadith about thirty years.

Key Takeaway
The Sunni tradition does not accept the Shi'a doctrine of twelve designated imams, dismissing it with astonishment.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When the author dismisses a deeply held belief of others with astonishment, how do you react when someone questions a core belief of your own? Do you examine the evidence or dismiss it? What would it take for you to honestly reconsider a cherished conviction?
?
The Shi'a claim a chain of designated imams. How do you know that your understanding of Islamic leadership is not based on similar assumptions without rigorous proof? Have you ever personally verified the basis of your own beliefs, or do you rely on what you've been taught without question?
?
The hadith about nine imams is considered unreliable by Sunni scholars. Are there reports or traditions you accept uncritically because they support your group's position? What would it cost you to scrutinize them, and are you willing to pay that price?

Consensus and Prophetic Proofs for Ali's Caliphate
Author defends Ali's caliphate using consensus and hadith.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that Sunni scholars affirm Ali's caliphate through both the consensus of the community and specific prophetic hadiths.

Core Idea
Ali's caliphate was established by community consensus and prophetic promise.

This passage opens by recounting that prominent Companions like Muhammad ibn Maslama were excused from fighting against Muslims due to hadiths, but the author quickly establishes that Ali's caliphate was legitimate. He cites three key hadiths and a consensus argument from the shura council, reinforcing that Ali was the rightful caliph after Uthman.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once to get the flow. Note the names mentioned and the hadiths cited.
2
Identify the three hadiths
Find the three prophetic traditions: the thirty-year caliphate, the prediction about Ali fighting three groups, and the hadith about Ammar being killed.
These hadiths are used as evidence for Ali's legitimacy.
3
Understand the consensus reasoning
Notice how the author explains that the shura council's decision was either for Uthman or Ali, and when Uthman died, Ali became the consensus choice.
Imam al-Haramayn's quote reinforces that no one denied Ali's imamate.
4
Reflect on the author's method
Consider how the author combines prophetic tradition and rational consensus to defend Ali's caliphate.
This is a typical Sunni approach.
